"""Custom CUDA top-k kernel for RTX PRO 6000 Blackwell.

Per-thread min-heap scan + float4 loads, sequential merge by thread 0.
Templated on K to avoid register spills for small k.
Transposed shared-memory write to avoid bank conflicts.
"""
import torch
import torch.nn as nn
from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline

CUDA_SRC = r"""
#include <torch/extension.h>
#include <c10/cuda/CUDAException.h>
#include <cfloat>
#include <cstdint>

// ---------- heap helpers, templated on K ---------- //

template<int K>
__device__ __forceinline__
void sift_up(float* v, int* idx, int pos) {
    while (pos > 0) {
        int p = (pos - 1) >> 1;
        if (v[pos] >= v[p]) break;
        float tv = v[pos]; v[pos] = v[p]; v[p] = tv;
        int ti = idx[pos]; idx[pos] = idx[p]; idx[p] = ti;
        pos = p;
    }
}

template<int K>
__device__ __forceinline__
void sift_down(float* v, int* idx, int sz, int pos) {
    while (true) {
        int l = (pos << 1) + 1;
        int r = l + 1;
        int s = pos;
        if (l < sz && l < K && v[l] < v[s]) s = l;
        if (r < sz && r < K && v[r] < v[s]) s = r;
        if (s == pos) break;
        float tv = v[pos]; v[pos] = v[s]; v[s] = tv;
        int ti = idx[pos]; idx[pos] = idx[s]; idx[s] = ti;
        pos = s;
    }
}

template<int K>
__device__ __forceinline__
void heap_try(float* v, int* idx, int& hs, float val, int id) {
    if (hs < K) {
        int p = hs++;
        v[p] = val; idx[p] = id;
        sift_up<K>(v, idx, p);
    } else if (val > v[0]) {
        v[0] = val; idx[0] = id;
        sift_down<K>(v, idx, K, 0);
    }
}

// ---------- main kernel ---------- //

template<int BLOCK, int K>
__global__ void __launch_bounds__(BLOCK)
topk_kernel(const float* __restrict__ input,
            float* __restrict__ out_vals,
            int64_t* __restrict__ out_idxs,
            int n)
{
    int row = blockIdx.x;
    int tid = threadIdx.x;
    const float* row_in = input + static_cast<int64_t>(row) * n;

    float lv[K];
    int li[K];
    int hs = 0;

    // Float4 vector loads
    const float4* row4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(row_in);
    int n4 = n >> 2;
    for (int i4 = tid; i4 < n4; i4 += BLOCK) {
        float4 f = row4[i4];
        int base = i4 << 2;
        heap_try<K>(lv, li, hs, f.x, base);
        heap_try<K>(lv, li, hs, f.y, base + 1);
        heap_try<K>(lv, li, hs, f.z, base + 2);
        heap_try<K>(lv, li, hs, f.w, base + 3);
    }
    int rem = n4 << 2;
    for (int i = rem + tid; i < n; i += BLOCK)
        heap_try<K>(lv, li, hs, row_in[i], i);
    for (int i = hs; i < K; i++) { lv[i] = -FLT_MAX; li[i] = -1; }

    // Write to shared memory (transposed for bank-conflict-free access)
    extern __shared__ char smem[];
    float* sv = reinterpret_cast<float*>(smem);
    int* si = reinterpret_cast<int*>(sv + BLOCK * K);

    for (int i = 0; i < K; i++) {
        sv[i * BLOCK + tid] = lv[i];
        si[i * BLOCK + tid] = li[i];
    }
    __syncthreads();

    // Thread 0: sequential merge of BLOCK*K candidates
    if (tid == 0) {
        float best[K];
        int best_i[K];
        int bs = 0;
        int total = BLOCK * K;
        for (int i = 0; i < total; i++)
            heap_try<K>(best, best_i, bs, sv[i], si[i]);
        for (int i = bs; i < K; i++) { best[i] = -FLT_MAX; best_i[i] = 0; }

        // Extract descending
        float* ov = out_vals + static_cast<int64_t>(row) * K;
        int64_t* oi = out_idxs + static_cast<int64_t>(row) * K;
        for (int i = K - 1; i >= 0; i--) {
            ov[i] = best[0];
            oi[i] = static_cast<int64_t>(best_i[0]);
            bs--;
            if (bs > 0) {
                best[0] = best[bs]; best_i[0] = best_i[bs];
                sift_down<K>(best, best_i, bs, 0);
            }
        }
    }
}

// ---------- launcher ---------- //

template<int BLOCK, int K>
void launch_typed(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor out_v, torch::Tensor out_i) {
    int B = x.size(0), N = x.size(1);
    int smem = BLOCK * K * (sizeof(float) + sizeof(int));
    cudaFuncSetAttribute(topk_kernel<BLOCK, K>,
        cudaFuncAttributeMaxDynamicSharedMemorySize, smem);
    topk_kernel<BLOCK, K><<<B, BLOCK, smem>>>(
        x.data_ptr<float>(), out_v.data_ptr<float>(),
        out_i.data_ptr<int64_t>(), N);
    C10_CUDA_CHECK(cudaGetLastError());
}

std::tuple<torch::Tensor, torch::Tensor> launch_topk(torch::Tensor x, int k) {
    TORCH_CHECK(x.dim() == 2 && x.scalar_type() == torch::kFloat32 && x.is_contiguous());
    int B = x.size(0), N = x.size(1);
    auto out_v = torch::empty({B, k}, x.options());
    auto out_i = torch::empty({B, k}, x.options().dtype(torch::kInt64));

    // Dispatch on k and block size
    // For k<=32: BLOCK=256. For k=64: BLOCK=128 (shared memory constraint ~99KB opt-in).
    switch (k) {
        case 1:  launch_typed<256, 1>(x, out_v, out_i); break;
        case 8:  launch_typed<256, 8>(x, out_v, out_i); break;
        case 16: launch_typed<256, 16>(x, out_v, out_i); break;
        case 32: launch_typed<256, 32>(x, out_v, out_i); break;
        case 64: launch_typed<128, 64>(x, out_v, out_i); break;
        default: TORCH_CHECK(false, "Unsupported k=", k);
    }
    return std::make_tuple(out_v, out_i);
}
"""

CPP_SRC = "std::tuple<torch::Tensor, torch::Tensor> launch_topk(torch::Tensor x, int k);"

_mod = load_inline(
    name="topk_ext_v6",
    cpp_sources=CPP_SRC,
    cuda_sources=CUDA_SRC,
    functions=["launch_topk"],
    with_cuda=True,
    extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"],
)

class Model(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, batch: int, n: int, k: int):
        super().__init__()
        self.k = k
        self.register_buffer("_dummy", torch.zeros(1))

    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor):
        return _mod.launch_topk(x, self.k)

batch = 64
n = 8192
k = 8

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.randn(batch, n, dtype=torch.float32)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [batch, n, k]
Key insight from ncu: the arrays `lv[64]`, `li[64]`, `best[64]`, `best_i[64]` are being spilled to local memory because they're declared as size 64 regardless of runtime k. For k=8, the compiler allocates 128+ registers and spills. I need to template on k to get proper register allocation.

1	"""Canonical shape sweep for TopK.
2	
3	Mix of:
4	  - decoder vocab top-k (single sequence, very large n, moderate k) — pure
5	    bandwidth test; the input read dominates everything.
6	  - prefill / batched attention top-k (many rows, moderate n, small k) — tests
7	    per-row parallelism and shared-memory bitonic networks.
8	  - non-power-of-2 n stress case — bitonic sort networks naturally want
9	    powers of two; this forces the agent to handle padding or partial sorts.
10	  - small-k limit — k=1 (argmax) is a degenerate but useful sanity case.
11	"""

1	"""SOTA reference for TopK: torch.topk itself.
2	
3	torch.topk dispatches to a CUB-backed kernel that uses radix-select for
4	moderate k and a tuned bitonic sort for small n. It is the bar the agent's
5	hand-rolled bitonic kernel must beat. There is no obvious vendor library that
6	does better on the (batch, n, k) shape mix we evaluate — Faiss BlockSelect is
7	specialized for k>=32 with much larger n, and CUB's DeviceSegmentedRadixSort
8	sorts the full row (overkill for top-k).
9	
10	This file is INTENTIONALLY allowed to call torch.topk because it is the SOTA
11	oracle, not the agent's submission. The agent's solution.py is forbidden from
12	using torch.topk (see problem.yaml.forbidden).
13	"""

20	    """Best-available top-k reference. x: (batch, n) fp32."""
21	    return torch.topk(x, k=k, dim=-1, largest=True, sorted=True)
22	
23	
24	def is_available() -> bool:
25	    return True

1	"""Correctness runner for TopK.
2	
3	Runs solution.Model vs reference.Model across all shapes in shapes.py, 3 seeds

7	     returned sorted descending, so positional comparison is well-defined.

1	name: 05_topk_bitonic
2	display_name: "TopK via Bitonic Sort"

6	# Top-k is dominated by the input read (small output, no reduction over k).
7	# Comparator-network FLOPs are not the bottleneck on real hardware, so we
8	# track them but score on bandwidth.

10	                                          # bitonic network is so cheap relative

Bitonic sort has a specific structure. Let me think more carefully. A bitonic sort of N elements: - For each sequence length size = 2, 4, 8, ..., N: - For each stride = size/2, size/4, ..., 1: - Compare elements at positions (i, i + stride) where they're in the right phase - If they're in the wrong order, swap The comparison direction alternates based on the position. Actually, for sorting in descending order (which is what we want for top-k), the bitonic sort works like this: ```cuda for (int k = 2; k <= N; k *= 2) { for (int j = k >> 1; j > 0; j >>= 1) { // Each thread handles multiple comparison operations for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < N/2; idx += blockDim.x) { int ij = idx ^ j; if (ij > idx) { if ((idx & k) == 0) { // Sort ascending in this segment -> for descending, swap if a < b if (smem_vals[idx] < smem_vals[ij]) swap(idx, ij); } else { // Sort descending in this segment -> for descending, swap if a > b if (smem_vals[idx] > smem_vals[ij]) swap(idx, ij); } } } __syncthreads(); } } ``` Hmm, the indexing is tricky. Let me use a simpler formulation. Actually, for top-k, I don't need a full sort. I just need to identify the k largest elements. Let me use a cleaner bitonic sort implementation: ```cuda void bitonic_sort(float* vals, int* idxs, int N, int tid, int nthreads) { for (int k = 2; k <= N; k <<= 1) { for (int j = k >> 1; j > 0; j >>= 1) { for (int i = tid; i < N / 2; i += nthreads) { int l = i ^ j; if (l > i) { bool dir = (i & k) == 0; if ((dir && vals[i] < vals[l]) || (!dir && vals[i] > vals[l])) { float tv = vals[i]; vals[i] = vals[l]; vals[l] = tv; int ti = idxs[i]; idxs[i] = idxs[l]; idxs[l] = ti; } } } __syncthreads(); } } } ``` Wait, this formulation assumes the index `i` ranges from 0 to N/2-1 and `l = i ^ j` gives the comparison partner. Let me verify: For N=4, k=2, j=1: - i=0: l=0^1=1. Compare 0 and 1. - i=1: l=1^1=0. l < i, skip. - So we compare (0,1). For N=4, k=4, j=2: - i=0: l=0^2=2. Compare 0 and 2. - i=1: l=1^2=3. Compare 1 and 3. Then j=1: - i=0: l=0^1=1. Compare 0 and 1. - i=1: l=1^1=0. l < i, skip. This gives the right bitonic sort structure. Let me use a cleaner formulation: ```cuda for (int k = 2; k <= N; k <<= 1) { for (int j = k >> 1; j > 0; j >>= 1) { for (int tid_local = threadIdx.x; tid_local < N / 2; tid_local += blockDim.x) { // Map tid_local to comparison index int i = tid_local; // Find the actual pair int pair_idx = (i / j) * 2 * j + (i % j); int a = pair_idx; int b = pair_idx + j; if (b < N) { // Determine sort direction based on which segment bool ascending = (a & k) != 0; // for descending sort, flip direction bool should_swap = ascending ? Actually, for random Gaussian data, after the thread has seen enough elements to fill its local top-k (i.e., after seeing k elements), subsequent elements have probability k/i of being in the top k seen so far (where i is the number of elements seen). For a memory-optimal implementation, we want to minimize computation while maximizing memory throughput. A heap-based approach with O(n log k) comparisons is optimal for top-k selection. Actually, you know what, let me just write the code and test it. I'll start with a simple correct implementation and iterate. Let me write the kernel using `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline`. Here's my code: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <c10/cuda/CUDAException.h> #include <float.h> #include <cstdint> // Min-heap operations for top-k selection // The heap stores (value, index) pairs with the MINIMUM at the root. // This allows us to efficiently check if a new element should replace the root. static inline __device__ void heap_swap(float* vals, int* idxs, int i, int j) { float tv = vals[i]; vals[i] = vals[j]; vals[j] = tv; int ti = idxs[i]; idxs[i] = idxs[j]; idxs[j] = ti; } static inline __device__ void heap_sift_down(float* vals, int* idxs, int k, int i) { while (true) { int left = 2 * i + 1; int right = 2 * i + 2; int smallest = i; if (left < k && vals[left] < vals[smallest]) smallest = left; if (right < k && vals[right] < vals[smallest]) smallest = right; if (smallest == i) break; heap_swap(vals, idxs, i, smallest); i = smallest; } } static inline __device__ void heap_replace_root(float* vals, int* idxs, int k, float v, int idx) { vals[0] = v; idxs[0] = idx; heap_sift_down(vals, idxs, k, 0); } template<int BLOCK_SIZE> __global__ void topk_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ out_values, int64_t* __restrict__ out_indices, int batch, int n, int k) { int row = blockIdx.x; if (row >= batch) return; int tid = threadIdx.x; const float* row_in = input + (int64_t)row * n; // Local top-k using min-heap in registers // Max k = 64 float local_vals[64]; int local_idxs[64]; int heap_size = 0; // Scan elements in strided fashion for coalesced reads for (int i = tid; i < n; i += BLOCK_SIZE) { float v = row_in[i]; if (heap_size < k) { // Insert into heap int pos = heap_size; local_vals[pos] = v; local_idxs[pos] = i; heap_size++; // Sift up while (pos > 0) { int parent = (pos - 1) / 2; if (local_vals[pos] < local_vals[parent]) { heap_swap(local_vals, local_idxs, pos, parent); pos = parent; } else { break; } } } else if (v > local_vals[0]) { // Replace root and sift down heap_replace_root(local_vals, local_idxs, k, v, i); } } // Fill remaining heap with -inf if heap_size < k for (int i = heap_size; i < k; i++) { local_vals[i] = -FLT_MAX; local_idxs[i] = 0; } // Write local top-k to shared memory for merge extern __shared__ char smem[]; float* smem_vals = reinterpret_cast<float*>(smem); int* smem_idxs = reinterpret_cast<int*>(smem + BLOCK_SIZE * k * sizeof(float)); for (int i = 0; i < k; i++) { smem_vals[tid * k + i] = local_vals[i]; smem_idxs[tid * k + i] = local_idxs[i]; } __syncthreads(); // Thread 0 merges all per-thread results if (tid == 0) { float best_vals[64]; int best_idxs[64]; int best_size = 0; int total = BLOCK_SIZE * k; for (int i = 0; i < total; i++) { float v = smem_vals[i]; if (v == -FLT_MAX) continue; if (best_size < k) { int pos = best_size; best_vals[pos] = v; best_idxs[pos] = smem_idxs[i]; best_size++; // Sift up while (pos > 0) { int parent = (pos - 1) / 2; if (best_vals[pos] < best_vals[parent]) { heap_swap(best_vals, best_idxs, pos, parent); pos = parent; } else break; } } else if (v > best_vals[0]) { heap_replace_root(best_vals, best_idxs, k, v, smem_idxs[i]); } } // Now extract from heap in sorted order // Need to sort the heap - extract min repeatedly // Since k ≤ 64, use selection sort float sorted_vals[64]; int sorted_idxs[64]; for (int i = k - 1; i >= 0; i--) { sorted_vals[i] = best_vals[0]; sorted_idxs[i] = best_idxs[0]; best_size--; if (best_size > 0) { best_vals[0] = best_vals[best_size]; best_idxs[0] = best_idxs[best_size]; heap_sift_down(best_vals, best_idxs, best_size, 0); } } // Write output float* out_v = out_values + (int64_t)row * k; int64_t* out_i = out_indices + (int64_t)row * k; for (int i = 0; i < k; i++) { out_v[i] = sorted_vals[i]; out_i[i] = (int64_t)sorted_idxs[i]; } } } ``` Wait, the shared memory size depends on k, which is a runtime parameter. I'm using `extern __shared__`, which means I need to specify the size at kernel launch time. That's fine. Let me implement this as a two-kernel approach: ```cuda // Kernel 1: Partial top-k scan // Each block handles a chunk of the row __global__ void topk_scan_kernel(...) { int row = blockIdx.y; int chunk_id = blockIdx.x; int num_chunks = gridDim.x; int chunk_size = (n + num_chunks - 1) / num_chunks; int start = chunk_id * chunk_size; int end = min(start + chunk_size, n); // ... scan [start, end) and write top-k to global memory } // Kernel 2: Merge partial results __global__ void topk_merge_kernel(...) { int row = blockIdx.x; // Read num_chunks * k partial results // Merge to get final top-k } ``` For the multi-row cases (batch >= 16), I can use a single kernel with one block per row. Here's my plan: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline CUDA_CODE = """ #include <torch/extension.h> ... // Kernel 1: Partial top-k scan // Each block handles a chunk of one row template <int BLOCK_SIZE, int MAX_K> __global__ void topk_partial_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ partial_vals, int* __restrict__ partial_idxs, int batch, int n, int k, int chunks_per_row, int chunk_size) { int row = blockIdx.y; int chunk = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int start = chunk * chunk_size; int end = min(start + chunk_size, n); const float* row_in = input + (int64_t)row * n; // Local min-heap float vals[MAX_K]; int idxs[MAX_K]; int heap_size = 0; // Scan chunk for (int i = start + tid; i < end; i += BLOCK_SIZE) { float v = row_in[i]; // heap insert or replace ... } // Write to global memory float* out_vals = partial_vals + ((int64_t)row * chunks_per_row + chunk) * k; int* out_idxs = partial_idxs + ((int64_t)row * chunks_per_row + chunk) * k; for (int i = 0; i < k; i++) { if (i < heap_size) { out_vals[i] = vals[i]; out_idxs[i] = idxs[i]; } else { out_vals[i] = -FLT_MAX; out_idxs[i] = -1; } } } // Kernel 2: Merge partial results using bitonic sort template <int BLOCK_SIZE, int MAX_K> __global__ void topk_merge_kernel( const float* __restrict__ partial_vals, const int* __restrict__ partial_idxs, float* __restrict__ out_values, int64_t* __restrict__ out_indices, int batch, int k, int total_candidates) { int row = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; // Load all candidates into shared memory extern __shared__ char smem[]; float* svals = (float*)smem; int* sidxs = (int*)(svals + total_candidates); for (int i = tid; i < total_candidates; i += BLOCK_SIZE) { svals[i] = partial_vals[(int64_t)row * total_candidates + i]; sidxs[i] = partial_idxs[(int64_t)row * total_candidates + i]; } __syncthreads(); // Bitonic sort (descending) // ... sort total_candidates elements ... __syncthreads(); // Write top k if (tid < k) { out_values[(int64_t)row * k + tid] = svals[tid]; out_indices[(int64_t)row * k + tid] = (int64_t)sidxs[tid]; } } // Host functions std::tuple<torch::Tensor, torch::Tensor> topk_cuda( torch::Tensor input, int k) { int batch = input.size(0); int n = input.size(1); // Determine chunks_per_row int target_blocks = 128; int chunks_per_row = max(1, (target_blocks + batch - 1) / batch); int chunk_size = (n + chunks_per_row - 1) / chunks_per_row; // Allocate partial results auto partial_vals = torch::empty({batch, chunks_per_row, k}, input.options()); auto partial_idxs = torch::empty({batch, chunks_per_row, k}, input.options().dtype(torch::kInt32)); // Launch kernel 1 dim3 grid(chunks_per_row, batch); topk_partial_kernel<256, 64><<<grid, 256>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), partial_vals.data_ptr<float>(), partial_idxs.data_ptr<int>(), batch, n, k, chunks_per_row, chunk_size); // Allocate output auto out_values = torch::empty({batch, k}, input.options()); auto out_indices = torch::empty({batch, k}, input.options().dtype(torch::kInt64)); // Launch kernel 2 int total_candidates = chunks_per_row * k; int smem_size = total_candidates * (sizeof(float) + sizeof(int)); topk_merge_kernel<256, 64><<<batch, 256, smem_size>>>( partial_vals.data_ptr<float>(), partial_idxs.data_ptr<int>(), out_values.data_ptr<float>(), out_indices.data_ptr<int64_t>(), batch, k, total_candidates); return {out_values, out_indices}; } """ CPP_CODE = """ std::tuple<torch::Tensor, torch::Tensor> topk_cuda(torch::Tensor input, int k); """ module = load_inline( name='topk_cuda', cpp_sources=CPP_CODE, cuda_sources=CUDA_CODE, functions=['topk_cuda'], with_cuda=True, ) ``` This is the high-level structure. const int chunk = blockIdx.x; const int tid = threadIdx.x; const int64_t row_offset = (int64_t)row * n; const int start = chunk * chunk_size; const int end = min(start + chunk_size, n); const int chunk_len = end - start; i < k; i += BLOCK_SIZE) { ov[i] = -FLT_MAX; oi[i] = -1; } return; } // Local min-heap for top-k float hv[MAX_K]; int hi[MAX_K]; int heap_size = 0; // Scan chunk elements in strided fashion for (int i = start + tid; i < end; i += BLOCK_SIZE) { float v = input[row_offset + i]; if (heap_size < k) { // Insert into heap (sift up) int pos = heap_size++; hv[pos] = v; hi[pos] = i; while (pos > 0) { int parent = (pos - 1) >> 1; if (hv[pos] < hv[parent]) { float tv = hv[pos]; hv[pos] = hv[parent]; hv[parent] = tv; int ti = hi[pos]; hi[pos] = hi[parent]; hi[parent] = ti; pos = parent; } else break; } } else if (v > hv[0]) { // Replace root (sift down) hv[0] = v; hi[0] = i; int pos = 0; while (true) { int left = (pos << 1) + 1; int right = left + 1; int smallest = pos; if (left < k && hv[left] < hv[smallest]) smallest = left; if (right < k && hv[right] < hv[smallest]) smallest = right; if (smallest == pos) break; float tv = hv[pos]; hv[pos] = hv[smallest]; hv[smallest] = tv; int ti = hi[pos]; hi[pos] = hi[smallest]; hi[smallest] = ti; pos = smallest; } } } // Fill remaining with -inf for (int i = heap_size; i < k; i++) { hv[i] = -FLT_MAX; hi[i] = -1; } // Write to global memory float* ov = partial_vals + ((int64_t)row * chunks_per_row + chunk) * k; int* oi = partial_idxs + ((int64_t)row * chunks_per_row + chunk) * k; Instead of per-thread heaps + merge, I'll use a **block-cooperative approach** where threads work together on a single shared top-k buffer. **Approach: Block-cooperative top-k with shared memory buffer** 1. Shared memory has a sorted buffer of k (value, index) pairs, initialized to -inf. 2. The input is divided into tiles of TILE_SIZE elements. 3. In each iteration: a. After all tiles are processed, the shared memory buffer contains the top-k. The problem with this approach is the synchronization for each insertion. Each insertion into the shared buffer requires a critical section. I can avoid this by having each thread collect its top-k locally, then doing the merge in batches. But that's what I was doing before. OK, let me try yet another approach: **Radix select**. **Radix select approach:** 1. Find the k-th largest value using radix-based partitioning 2. Scan the input again to find all values >= the k-th value 3. Sort the top-k values This avoids the O(n log k) comparisons of the heap approach. Instead, it's O(n * num_bits) for the radix selection. For fp32 with 32-bit values, radix select typically uses 4-bit or 8-bit digits, requiring 8 or 4 passes over the data. For n=131072, k=64: - 4 passes of reading 512KB = 2MB total reads - Each pass: histogram + prefix sum + scatter - This is essentially a radix sort, just stopping early This approach is used by CUB's DeviceSelect and PyTorch's internal top-k implementation. It's good for large n and small k. But implementing radix select correctly for fp32 is tricky (need to handle sign bit, infinity, NaN, etc.). Let me look at what PyTorch does internally. Let me try a simpler approach: **Bitonic merge within a warp (no sync needed)** Within a warp, 32 threads each have k values. Total 32*k values. We want top k. Each pair has 2k values, take top k. - Round 2: Merge pairs of merged results (0,1), (2,3), ..., (14,15). Each has 2k values, take top k. - ... 5 rounds total (log2(32)=5). Each round uses warp shuffle to exchange values between threads. The merge of two sorted lists of k values into top k can be done in O(k) time using a standard two-pointer merge. Here's my implementation plan: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> #include <c10/cuda/CUDAException.h> #include <cfloat> template<int BLOCK_SIZE> __global__ void topk_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ out_values, int64_t* __restrict__ out_indices, int batch, int n, int k ) { int row = blockIdx.x; if (row >= batch) return; int tid = threadIdx.x; const float* row_in = input + (int64_t)row * n; // Per-thread local top-k (max k=64) float lv[64]; int li[64]; int heap_sz = 0; // Scan elements in strided coalesced fashion for (int i = tid; i < n; i += BLOCK_SIZE) { float v = row_in[i]; if (heap_sz < k) { // Sift up (min-heap) int pos = heap_sz++; lv[pos] = v; li[pos] = i; while (pos > 0) { int p = (pos - 1) >> 1; if (lv[pos] < lv[p]) { float tv = lv[pos]; lv[pos] = lv[p]; lv[p] = tv; int ti = li[pos]; li[pos] = li[p]; li[p] = ti; pos = p; } else break; } } else if (v > lv[0]) { lv[0] = v; li[0] = i; // Sift down int pos = 0; while (true) { int l = (pos << 1) + 1; int r = l + 1; int s = pos; if (l < k && lv[l] < lv[s]) s = l; if (r < k && lv[r] < lv[s]) s = r; if (s == pos) break; float tv = lv[pos]; lv[pos] = lv[s]; lv[s] = tv; int ti = li[pos]; li[pos] = li[s]; li[s] = ti; pos = s; } } } // Pad with -inf for (int i = heap_sz; i < k; i++) { lv[i] = -FLT_MAX; li[i] = -1; } // Write to shared memory // Total candidates: BLOCK_SIZE * k (power of 2 since BLOCK_SIZE=256=2^8, k is 1,8,16,32,64=2^i) // BLOCK_SIZE * k = 2^(8+i) extern __shared__ char smem[]; float* sv = (float*)smem; int* si = (int*)(sv + BLOCK_SIZE * k); for (int i = 0; i < k; i++) { sv[tid * k + i] = lv[i]; si[tid * k + i] = li[i]; } __syncthreads(); // Bitonic sort (descending) of BLOCK_SIZE * k elements int N = BLOCK_SIZE * k; for (int size = 2; size <= N; size <<= 1) { for (int stride = size >> 1; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { for (int idx = tid; idx < N / 2; idx += BLOCK_SIZE) { int a = idx; int b = a ^ stride; if (b > a) { // Determine direction: descending sort bool descending = (a & size) == 0; out_values[row_off + tid] = sv[tid]; out_indices[row_off + tid] = (int64_t)si[tid]; } } std::tuple<torch::Tensor, torch::Tensor> topk_cuda(torch::Tensor x, int k) { int batch = x.size(0); int n = x.size(1); auto out_values = torch::empty({batch, k}, x.options()); auto out_indices = torch::empty({batch, k}, x.options().dtype(torch::kInt64)); int block_size = 256; int smem_size = block_size * k * (sizeof(float) + sizeof(int)); topk_kernel<256><<<batch, block_size, smem_size>>>( x.data_ptr<float>(), out_values.data_ptr<float>(), out_indices.data_ptr<int64_t>(), batch, n, k ); C10_CUDA_CHECK(cudaGetLastError()); return {out_values, out_indices}; } """ module = load_inline(...) class Model(nn.Module): ... ``` Wait, I need to be careful with the bitonic sort indexing. The standard bitonic sort for N elements uses pairs (a, a ^ stride) where a ranges such that a < a ^ stride. The condition `b > a` ensures we only process each pair once. But there's a subtlety: `a` should range from 0 to N-1, and we process pairs where `a < (a ^ stride)` and `(a & size)` determines the direction. However, the typical formulation iterates over all N/2 pairs in each step. Let me use a known-correct bitonic sort formulation. The standard bitonic sort for descending order: For N elements, indexed 0 to N-1: ``` for k = 2, 4, 8, ..., N: for j = k/2, k/4, ..., 1: for all i in parallel where i < N/2: ij = i XOR j if ij > i: if (i AND k) == 0: // First half of the k-segment: sort descending if data[i] < data[ij]: swap(i, ij) else: // Second half: sort ascending if data[i] > data[ij]: swap(i, ij) ``` After the sort, the first k elements are the k largest in descending order. In the standard bitonic sort network: For step (k, j): - For each pair of indices (low, high) where: - low and high differ in the j-th bit position - low < high - low and high are in the same k-sized group The pairs are: - low = 2*m*j + (m % j) + offset, high = low + j - where m ranges over all valid values Actually, this is getting confusing. Let me use a simple, known-correct bitonic sort implementation: ```cuda // Bitonic sort N elements in shared memory in descending order // N must be a power of 2 void bitonic_sort_desc(float* sv, int* si, int N, int tid, int nthreads) { for (int k = 2; k <= N; k <<= 1) { for (int j = k >> 1; j > 0; j >>= 1) { // Process all N/2 comparison pairs in parallel for (int pair = tid; pair < N >> 1; pair += nthreads) { // Compute the two indices of this pair int group = pair / j; // which group of 2*j elements int pos = pair % j; // position within the group int low = group * 2 * j + pos; int high = low + j; // Determine direction: first half of k-group is descending bool descending = (low & k) == 0; bool swap; if (descending) { swap = sv[low] < sv[high]; // want low >= high } else { swap = sv[low] > sv[high]; // want low <= high } if (swap) { float tv = sv[low]; sv[low] = sv[high]; sv[high] = tv; int ti = si[low]; si[low] = si[high]; si[high] = ti; } } __syncthreads(); } } } ``` Let me verify with N=4, initial [3, 1, 4, 2]: k=2, j=1: - N/2=2 pairs. - pair=0: group=0/1=0, pos=0%1=0. low=0, high=1. descending=(0&2)==0 → true. sv[0]=3, sv[1]=1. 3<1? Actually, for merging T sorted (or heap) lists of k elements into a single top-k: - After the heap is full, we process (T-1)*k remaining candidates - Each candidate is from a different thread's top-k, so it's a "local winner" - The probability that a local winner beats the current global k-th value depends on the data distribution - For uniform random data: each thread's top-k has elements from the top k/m fraction (where m = n/T = elements per thread) - The probability that a given thread's j-th largest element is in the global top-k is approximately k/(m*T) = k/n - The expected number of insertions is T * sum_{j=1}^{k} min(1, k/(j*T)) ≈ T * k * (1/T) * H(k) ≈ k * ln(k) for large T - For T=256, k=64: k * ln(k) = 64 * 4.16 ≈ 266 OK so roughly 266 insertions out of 16320 remaining candidates. Let me re-read the problem description: "The input is a 2D fp32 tensor and you return the top-k values and their int64 indices along the last dim, sorted descending, same contract as torch.topk(largest=True, sorted=True)." So the output must be sorted descending. My heap approach gives me the top-k in arbitrary order (heap property, not sorted). I need to sort them. For the sequential merge approach, the heap is a min-heap of size k. To extract in descending order: 1. Extract min, place at position k-1 2. Extract min, place at position k-2 3. ... until heap is empty This gives descending order. Cost: k * log(k) comparisons. For k=64: 64 * 6 = 384 ≈ 0.4μs. For the bitonic sort approach, the result is already sorted. ✓ OK, let me code it now. I'll implement the sequential merge approach first (simpler) and add bitonic sort later. Here's the code: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <c10/cuda/CUDAException.h> #include <cfloat> #include <cstdint> template<int BLOCK_SIZE> __global__ void topk_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ out_values, int64_t* __restrict__ out_indices, int batch, int n, int k ) { int row = blockIdx.x; if (row >= batch) return; int tid = threadIdx.x; const float* row_in = input + static_cast<int64_t>(row) * n; // Per-thread local min-heap (max 64 elements) // Using register arrays float lv[64]; int li[64]; int hs = 0; // heap size // Scan elements for (int i = tid; i < n; i += BLOCK_SIZE) { float v = row_in[i]; if (hs < k) { int pos = hs++; lv[pos] = v; li[pos] = i; while (pos > 0) { int p = (pos - 1) >> 1; if (lv[pos] < lv[p]) { float tv = lv[pos]; lv[pos] = lv[p]; lv[p] = tv; int ti = li[pos]; li[pos] = li[p]; li[p] = ti; pos = p; } else break; } } else if (v > lv[0]) { lv[0] = v; li[0] = i; int pos = 0; while (true) { int l = (pos << 1) + 1, r = l + 1, s = pos; if (l < k && lv[l] < lv[s]) s = l; if (r < k && lv[r] < lv[s]) s = r; if (s == pos) break; float tv = lv[pos]; lv[pos] = lv[s]; lv[s] = tv; int ti = li[pos]; li[pos] = li[s]; li[s] = ti; pos = s; } } } // Pad unused slots with -inf for (int i = hs; i < k; i++) { lv[i] = -FLT_MAX; li[i] = -1; } // Write to shared memory extern __shared__ char smem[]; float* sv = reinterpret_cast<float*>(smem); int* si = reinterpret_cast<int*>(sv + BLOCK_SIZE * k); for (int i = 0; i < k; i++) { sv[tid * k + i] = lv[i]; si[tid * k + i] = li[i]; } __syncthreads(); // Thread 0 merges all BLOCK_SIZE * k candidates if (tid == 0) { // Build a min-heap of size k from all candidates float best[64]; int best_i[64]; int bhs = 0; int total = BLOCK_SIZE * k; for (int i = 0; i < total; i++) { float v = sv[i]; if (v == -FLT_MAX) continue; if (bhs < k) { int pos = bhs++; best[pos] = v; best_i[pos] = si[i]; while (pos > 0) { int p = (pos - 1) >> 1; if (best[pos] < best[p]) { float tv = best[pos]; best[pos] = best[p]; best[p] = tv; int ti = best_i[pos]; best_i[pos] = best_i[p]; best_i[p] = ti; pos = p; } else break; } } else if (v > best[0]) { best[0] = v; best_i[0] = si[i]; int pos = 0; while (true) { int l = (pos << 1) + 1, r = l + 1, s = pos; if (l < k && best[l] < best[s]) s = l; if (r < k && best[r] < best[s]) s = r; if (s == pos) break; float tv = best[pos]; best[pos] = best[s]; best[s] = tv; int ti = best_i[pos]; best_i[pos] = best_i[s]; best_i[s] = ti; pos = s; } } } // Fill remaining with -inf for (int i = bhs; i < k; i++) { best[i] = -FLT_MAX; best_i[i] = 0; } // Extract in descending order float* ov = out_values + static_cast<int64_t>(row) * k; int64_t* oi = out_indices + static_cast<int64_t>(row) * k; for (int i = k - 1; i >= 0; i--) { ov[i] = best[0]; oi[i] = static_cast<int64_t>(best_i[0]); bhs--; if (bhs > 0) { best[0] = best[bhs]; best_i[0] = best_i[bhs]; int pos = 0; while (true) { int l = (pos << 1) + 1, r = l + 1, s = pos; if (l < bhs && best[l] < best[s]) s = l; if (r < bhs && best[r] < best[s]) s = r; if (s == pos) break; float tv = best[pos]; best[pos] = best[s]; best[s] = tv; int ti = best_i[pos]; best_i[pos] = best_i[s]; best_i[s] = ti; pos = s; } } } } } std::tuple<torch::Tensor, torch::Tensor> topk_cuda(torch::Tensor x, int k) { TORCH_CHECK(x.dim() == 2, "Expected 2D input"); TORCH_CHECK(x.scalar_type() == torch::kFloat32, "Expected fp32"); int batch = x.size(0); int n = x.size(1); auto opts = x.options(); auto out_values = torch::empty({batch, k}, opts); auto out_indices = torch::empty({batch, k}, opts.dtype(torch::kInt64)); constexpr int BLOCK = 256; int smem = BLOCK * k * (sizeof(float) + sizeof(int)); topk_kernel<BLOCK><<<batch, BLOCK, smem>>>( x.data_ptr<float>(), out_values.data_ptr<float>(), out_indices.data_ptr<int64_t>(), batch, n, k ); C10_CUDA_CHECK(cudaGetLastError()); return std::make_tuple(out_values, out_indices); } """ ``` This should work. Let me try a warp-level approach to avoid syncs. **Warp-level merge:** Phase 1: Within each warp (32 threads), merge 32*k candidates to k using warp shuffle. Algorithm for warp merge of 32 sorted lists of k elements: - Use a bitonic merge network with warp shuffles - 5 rounds (log2(32)=5), each round merges pairs of lists For each round r (r = 0, 1, 2, 3, 4): - Partner = tid ^ (1 << r) - If tid < partner: this thread is the "low" thread, keeps top k - Merge local k values with partner's k values, keep top k - If tid > partner: this thread is the "high" thread, becomes idle (or keeps bottom k) For each merge: thread receives k values from partner via shuffle, merges two sorted lists of k into top k. Merge of two sorted lists of k into top k: O(k) using standard two-pointer merge. For each round: k shuffles + k comparisons ≈ k * 6ns. 5 rounds: 5 * k * 6ns = 30k ns. For k=64: 1920ns ≈ 1.9μs For k=32: 960ns ≈ 1μs For k=16: 480ns ≈ 0.5μs For k=8: 240ns ≈ 0.24μs For k=1: 30ns ≈ 0.03μs Phase 2: Write per-warp results (4 warps, each with k values) to shared memory. One __syncthreads(). Then thread 0 merges 4*k candidates. Inter-warp merge (sequential by thread 0): - 4 * k candidates, merge into heap of k - For k=64: 256 * 1 + ~20 * 6 ≈ 376 ≈ 0.4μs - For k=32: 128 * 1 + ~10 * 5 ≈ 178 ≈ 0.2μs - For k=16: 64 * 1 + ~5 * 4 ≈ 84 ≈ 0.1μs - For k=8: 32 * 1 ≈ 0.05μs - For k=1: 4 * 1 ≈ 0.01μs Total merge: - k=64: 1.9 + 0.4 = 2.3μs (vs 10μs sequential, 7.5μs bitonic) - k=32: 1.0 + 0.2 = 1.2μs (vs 5μs sequential, 5.1μs bitonic) - k=16: 0.5 + 0.1 = 0.6μs (vs 2.3μs sequential) - k=8: 0.24 + 0.05 = 0.3μs (vs 1.1μs sequential) - k=1: 0.03 + 0.01 = 0.04μs (vs 0.1μs sequential) The warp-level merge is significantly faster! Let me use this approach. One more optimization I want to add: for the scan, use float4 loads to improve memory throughput. Actually, for the heap-based scan, using float4 means processing 4 elements per iteration. The heap operations are: - Compare with root (1 comparison) - If larger, sift down (~log k comparisons) For 4 elements, that's 4 root comparisons + ~4*P(insert)*log(k) sift downs. The float4 load gives 4x memory throughput improvement, which is significant for memory-bound kernels. Let me add float4 loads: ```cuda // Process 4 elements at a time int n4 = (n / 4) * 4; // Round down to multiple of 4 for (int i4 = tid; i4 * 4 < n4; i4 += BLOCK_SIZE) { float4 vec = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(row_in)[i4]; process(vec.x, i4 * 4 + 0); process(vec.y, i4 * 4 + 1); process(vec.z, i4 * 4 + 2); process(vec.w, i4 * 4 + 3); } // Handle remainder for (int i = n4 + tid; i < n; i += BLOCK_SIZE) { process(row_in[i], i); } ``` Wait, the indexing is wrong. Let me fix: ```cuda const float4* row_in4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(row_in); int n4 = n / 4; for (int i4 = tid; i4 < n4; i4 += BLOCK_SIZE) { float4 vec = row_in4[i4]; process(vec.x, i4 * 4 + 0); process(vec.y, i4 * 4 + 1); process(vec.z, i4 * 4 + 2); process(vec.w, i4 * 4 + 3); } // Remainder for (int i = n4 * 4 + tid; i < n; i += BLOCK_SIZE) { process(row_in[i], i); } ``` This should give coalesced float4 reads. Each thread reads 4 consecutive floats, and threads read consecutive float4s. Actually, the alignment of the float4 reads might be an issue. PyTorch tensors are typically 256-byte aligned, so float4 reads from the start should be fine. Let me add this optimization. OK let me write the final code now. I'll implement: 1. Thread-local min-heap scan with float4 loads 2. Sequential merge by thread 0 3. Extract sorted output from heap Then I'll test correctness and measure performance. After that, I'll optimize with warp-level merge. Let me code this up. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline CUDA_SRC = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> #include <c10/cuda/CUDAException.h> #include <cfloat> #include <cstdint> // Inline helpers for min-heap operations on register arrays __device__ __forceinline__ void heap_sift_up(float* v, int* idx, int pos) { while (pos > 0) { int p = (pos - 1) >> 1; if (v[pos] < v[p]) { float tv = v[pos]; v[pos] = v[p]; v[p] = tv; int ti = idx[pos]; idx[pos] = idx[p]; idx[p] = ti; pos = p; } else break; } } __device__ __forceinline__ void heap_sift_down(float* v, int* idx, int size, int pos) { while (true) { int l = (pos << 1) + 1; int r = l + 1; int s = pos; if (l < size && v[l] < v[s]) s = l; if (r < size && v[r] < v[s]) s = r; if (s == pos) break; float tv = v[pos]; v[pos] = v[s]; v[s] = tv; int ti = idx[pos]; idx[pos] = idx[s]; idx[s] = ti; pos = s; } } __device__ __forceinline__ void heap_insert(float* v, int* idx, int& size, int k, float val, int id) { if (size < k) { v[size] = val; idx[size] = id; size++; heap_sift_up(v, idx, size - 1); } else if (val > v[0]) { v[0] = val; idx[0] = id; heap_sift_down(v, idx, k, 0); } } template<int BLOCK> __global__ void topk_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ out_vals, int64_t* __restrict__ out_idxs, int batch, int n, int k ) { int row = blockIdx.x; if (row >= batch) return; int tid = threadIdx.x; const float* row_in = input + static_cast<int64_t>(row) * n; // Local min-heap float lv[64]; int li[64]; int hs = 0; // Process with float4 loads const float4* row4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(row_in); int n4 = n >> 2; for (int i4 = tid; i4 < n4; i4 += BLOCK) { float4 v = row4[i4]; int base = i4 << 2; heap_insert(lv, li, hs, k, v.x, base); heap_insert(lv, li, hs, k, v.y, base + 1); heap_insert(lv, li, hs, k, v.z, base + 2); heap_insert(lv, li, hs, k, v.w, base + 3); } // Remainder for (int i = n4 * 4 + tid; i < n; i += BLOCK) { heap_insert(lv, li, hs, k, row_in[i], i); } // Pad for (int i = hs; i < k; i++) { lv[i] = -FLT_MAX; li[i] = -1; } // Write to shared memory for merge extern __shared__ char smem[]; float* sv = reinterpret_cast<float*>(smem); int* si = reinterpret_cast<int*>(sv + BLOCK * k); for (int i = 0; i < k; i++) { sv[tid * k + i] = lv[i]; si[tid * k + i] = li[i]; } __syncthreads(); // Thread 0 merges all BLOCK * k candidates if (tid == 0) { float best[64]; int best_i[64]; int bs = 0; int total = BLOCK * k; for (int i = 0; i < total; i++) { float v = sv[i]; if (v <= -FLT_MAX) continue; heap_insert(best, best_i, bs, k, v, si[i]); } // Pad for (int i = bs; i < k; i++) { best[i] = -FLT_MAX; best_i[i] = 0; } // Extract in descending order (pop min from heap repeatedly) float* ov = out_vals + static_cast<int64_t>(row) * k; int64_t* oi = out_idxs + static_cast<int64_t>(row) * k; for (int i = k - 1; i >= 0; i--) { ov[i] = best[0]; oi[i] = static_cast<int64_t>(best_i[0]); bs--; if (bs > 0) { best[0] = best[bs]; best_i[0] = best_i[bs]; heap_sift_down(best, best_i, bs, 0); } } } } std::tuple<torch::Tensor, torch::Tensor> launch_topk(torch::Tensor x, int k) { TORCH_CHECK(x.dim() == 2 && x.scalar_type() == torch::kFloat32); int B = x.size(0), N = x.size(1); auto out_v = torch::empty({B, k}, x.options()); auto out_i = torch::empty({B, k}, x.options().dtype(torch::kInt64)); constexpr int BLOCK = 256; int smem = BLOCK * k * (sizeof(float) + sizeof(int)); topk_kernel<BLOCK><<<B, BLOCK, smem>>>( x.data_ptr<float>(), out_v.data_ptr<float>(), out_i.data_ptr<int64_t>(), B, N, k ); C10_CUDA_CHECK(cudaGetLastError()); return std::make_tuple(out_v, out_i); } """ CPP_SRC = "std::tuple<torch::Tensor, torch::Tensor> launch_topk(torch::Tensor x, int k);" _mod = load_inline( name="topk_ext", cpp_sources=CPP_SRC, cuda_sources=CUDA_SRC, functions=["launch_topk"], with_cuda=True, extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3"], ) class Model(nn.Module): def __init__(self, batch, n, k): super().__init__() self.k = k self.register_buffer("_dummy", torch.zeros(1)) def forward(self, x): values, indices = _mod.launch_topk(x, self.k) return values, indices batch = 64 n = 8192 k = 8 def get_inputs(): return [torch.randn(batch, n, dtype=torch.float32)] def get_init_inputs(): return [batch, n, k] ``` Wait, there's an issue with `load_inline`. The shared memory for k=64 with 256 threads: 256 * 64 * 8 = 128KB. This might exceed the default shared memory limit. Let me check: on CUDA, the default maximum dynamic shared memory per block is usually 48KB. But with `cudaFuncSetAttribute`, it can be increased. In PyTorch's `load_inline`, I don't have direct access to the kernel function handle to set this attribute. Let me use `cudaFuncSetAttribute` in the host code. Actually, I can do it in C++: ```cpp // Before kernel launch cudaFuncSetAttribute(topk_kernel<BLOCK>, cudaFuncAttributeMaxDynamicSharedMemorySize, smem); ``` Or I can reduce the shared memory requirement. For k=64, I could use 64 threads instead of 256: - Shared memory: 64 * 64 * 8 = 32KB. Fine. - But each thread handles 131072/64 = 2048 elements. More per-thread work. Or 128 threads: - Shared memory: 128 * 64 * 8 = 64KB. - Need to check if 64KB is within the default limit. Actually, on modern NVIDIA GPUs (Ampere+), the default shared memory per block is 48KB, but the driver can be configured to allow more. PyTorch typically handles this internally. For n=12000, BLOCK=256: each thread handles 47 elements. Fine. For n=131072, BLOCK=256: each thread handles 512 elements. Fine. All good. Actually wait, I realized there's another potential issue. First k candidates fill the heap. Some might be -inf. 2. Remaining candidates: compare with root. If larger, replace root and sift down. Since -FLT_MAX < any real value, all real values will eventually replace -inf in the heap (assuming there are at least k real values total, which is true when n >= k). +"""Custom CUDA top-k kernel for RTX PRO 6000 Blackwell.
+
+Strategy: per-thread min-heap scan with float4 vector loads,
+warp-shuffle hierarchical merge, shared-memory inter-warp merge.
+"""
+import torch
+import torch.nn as nn
+from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline
+
+CUDA_SRC = r"""
+#include <torch/extension.h>
+#include <c10/cuda/CUDAException.h>
+#include <cfloat>
+#include <cstdint>
+
+// ---------- heap helpers (register arrays, max size 64) ---------- //
+
+__device__ __forceinline__
+void sift_up(float* v, int* idx, int pos) {
+    while (pos > 0) {
+        int p = (pos - 1) >> 1;
+        if (v[pos] >= v[p]) break;
+        float tv = v[pos]; v[pos] = v[p]; v[p] = tv;
+        int ti = idx[pos]; idx[pos] = idx[p]; idx[p] = ti;
+        pos = p;
+    }
+}
+
+__device__ __forceinline__
+void sift_down(float* v, int* idx, int sz, int pos) {
+    while (true) {
+        int l = (pos << 1) + 1;
+        int r = l + 1;
+        int s = pos;
+        if (l < sz && v[l] < v[s]) s = l;
+        if (r < sz && v[r] < v[s]) s = r;
+        if (s == pos) break;
+        float tv = v[pos]; v[pos] = v[s]; v[s] = tv;
+        int ti = idx[pos]; idx[pos] = idx[s]; idx[s] = ti;
+        pos = s;
+    }
+}
+
+// Insert into min-heap of capacity k (actual size tracked by *hs).
+__device__ __forceinline__
+void heap_try(float* v, int* idx, int* hs, int k, float val, int id) {
+    if (*hs < k) {
+        int p = (*hs)++;
+        v[p] = val; idx[p] = id;
+        sift_up(v, idx, p);
+    } else if (val > v[0]) {
+        v[0] = val; idx[0] = id;
+        sift_down(v, idx, k, 0);
+    }
+}
+
+// ---------- main kernel ---------- //
+
+template<int BLOCK>
+__global__ void topk_kernel(
+    const float* __restrict__ input,
+    float* __restrict__ out_vals,
+    int64_t* __restrict__ out_idxs,
+    int batch, int n, int k)
+{
+    int row = blockIdx.x;
+    if (row >= batch) return;
+
+    int tid = threadIdx.x;
+    const float* row_in = input + static_cast<int64_t>(row) * n;
+
+    // ---- Phase 1: per-thread min-heap scan ---- //
+    float lv[64];
+    int li[64];
+    int hs = 0;
+
+    // Vectorised (float4) reads
+    const float4* row4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(row_in);
+    int n4 = n >> 2;
+    for (int i4 = tid; i4 < n4; i4 += BLOCK) {
+        float4 f = row4[i4];
+        int base = i4 << 2;
+        heap_try(lv, li, &hs, k, f.x, base);
+        heap_try(lv, li, &hs, k, f.y, base + 1);
+        heap_try(lv, li, &hs, k, f.z, base + 2);
+        heap_try(lv, li, &hs, k, f.w, base + 3);
+    }
+    // Remainder
+    int rem_start = n4 << 2;
+    for (int i = rem_start + tid; i < n; i += BLOCK) {
+        heap_try(lv, li, &hs, k, row_in[i], i);
+    }
+
+    // Pad unused slots
+    for (int i = hs; i < k; i++) { lv[i] = -FLT_MAX; li[i] = -1; }
+
+    // ---- Phase 2: warp-level hierarchical merge ---- //
+    // Each warp reduces its 32*k candidates to k via pairwise merge.
+    // Uses __shfl_sync to exchange k values between partner threads.
+
+    int lane = tid & 31;
+    int warp = tid >> 5;
+    int num_warps = BLOCK >> 5;
+
+    // Merge within warp: 5 rounds of pairwise merge for 32 threads.
+    // Thread i merges with thread (i ^ (1<<r)).
+    // The "lower" thread keeps the merged top-k.
+    for (int round = 0; round < 5; round++) {
+        int partner = lane ^ (1 << round);
+        // lower thread keeps result
+        bool keep = lane < partner;
+        // Receive partner's k values via shuffle
+        float pv[64];
+        int pi[64];
+        for (int j = 0; j < k; j++) {
+            pv[j] = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, lv[j], partner);
+            pi[j] = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, li[j], partner);
+        }
+        if (keep) {
+            // Merge two sorted desc lists (lv and pv) keeping top k.
+            // lv and pv are min-heaps; we can just insert all partner values
+            // into our heap and let the heap naturally keep top k.
+            // But faster: since both are heaps, insert pv elements.
+            for (int j = 0; j < k; j++) {
+                if (pv[j] > lv[0]) {
+                    lv[0] = pv[j]; li[0] = pi[j];
+                    sift_down(lv, li, k, 0);
+                }
+            }
+        }
+    }
+
+    // ---- Phase 3: inter-warp merge via shared memory ---- //
+    extern __shared__ char smem[];
+    float* sv = reinterpret_cast<float*>(smem);
+    int* si = reinterpret_cast<int*>(sv + num_warps * k);
+
+    // Lane 0 of each warp writes its top-k to shared memory
+    if (lane == 0) {
+        for (int j = 0; j < k; j++) {
+            sv[warp * k + j] = lv[j];
+            si[warp * k + j] = li[j];
+        }
+    }
+    __syncthreads();
+
+    // Thread 0 merges all warp results sequentially
+    if (tid == 0) {
+        float best[64];
+        int best_i[64];
+        int bs = 0;
+
+        int total = num_warps * k;
+        for (int i = 0; i < total; i++) {
+            heap_try(best, best_i, &bs, k, sv[i], si[i]);
+        }
+        for (int i = bs; i < k; i++) { best[i] = -FLT_MAX; best_i[i] = 0; }
+
+        // Extract in descending order (pop min repeatedly)
+        float* ov = out_vals + static_cast<int64_t>(row) * k;
+        int64_t* oi = out_idxs + static_cast<int64_t>(row) * k;
+        for (int i = k - 1; i >= 0; i--) {
+            ov[i] = best[0];
+            oi[i] = static_cast<int64_t>(best_i[0]);
+            bs--;
+            if (bs > 0) {
+                best[0] = best[bs]; best_i[0] = best_i[bs];
+                sift_down(best, best_i, bs, 0);
+            }
+        }
+    }
+}
+
+// ---------- host launcher ---------- //
+
+std::tuple<torch::Tensor, torch::Tensor> launch_topk(torch::Tensor x, int k) {

In the standard bitonic merge network: - Round 0: Thread 0 merges with thread 1, thread 2 with thread 3, etc. - Thread 0 keeps merged result, thread 1 is "eliminated" - Round 1: Thread 0 merges with thread 2, thread 4 with thread 6, etc. - Thread 0 keeps merged result, thread 2 is "eliminated" - ... If some lanes are in the `if` branch and others in the `else` branch, and both have __shfl_sync, the hardware will execute them correctly because: Actually, GPU warp execution is SIMT. When lanes diverge: 1. Lanes with keep=true execute the if-branch. Lanes with keep=false are masked off. 2. In the if-branch, there's a __shfl_sync. All lanes (including masked ones) participate. 3. 16	// ---------- heap helpers (register arrays, max size 64) ---------- //
17	
18	__device__ __forceinline__
19	void sift_up(float* v, int* idx, int pos) {
20	    while (pos > 0) {
21	        int p = (pos - 1) >> 1;
22	        if (v[pos] >= v[p]) break;
23	        float tv = v[pos]; v[pos] = v[p]; v[p] = tv;
24	        int ti = idx[pos]; idx[pos] = idx[p]; idx[p] = ti;
25	        pos = p;
26	    }
27	}
28	
29	__device__ __forceinline__
30	void sift_down(float* v, int* idx, int sz, int pos) {
31	    while (true) {
32	        int l = (pos << 1) + 1;
33	        int r = l + 1;
34	        int s = pos;
35	        if (l < sz && v[l] < v[s]) s = l;
36	        if (r < sz && v[r] < v[s]) s = r;
37	        if (s == pos) break;
38	        float tv = v[pos]; v[pos] = v[s]; v[s] = tv;
39	        int ti = idx[pos]; idx[pos] = idx[s]; idx[s] = ti;
40	        pos = s;
41	    }
42	}
43	
44	// Insert into min-heap of capacity k (actual size tracked by *hs).
45	__device__ __forceinline__

In the scan kernel, I have: ```cuda const float4* r4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(row_in + start); ``` But `row_in + start` might not be 16-byte aligned! If `start` is not a multiple of 4, the float4 load will be misaligned. For chunks_per_row=128, chunk_size=1024: start = chunk * 1024. 1024 is a multiple of 4. So start is always a multiple of 4, and row_in + start is aligned to 4 floats = 16 bytes. ✓ Wait, but what about the base calculation in the heap_try calls? ```cuda int base = start + (i4 << 2); ``` This should be correct. Hmm, let me look more carefully at the scan kernel. Actually, I think I see a potential issue: ```cuda for (int i4 = tid; i4 < n4; i4 += BLOCK) { float4 f = r4[i4]; ``` Here, `r4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(row_in + start)`. And `n4 = len >> 2` where `len = end - start = chunk_size`. But `r4[i4]` reads from `row_in + start + i4*4`. And `i4 = tid, tid + BLOCK, tid + 2*BLOCK, ...`. So the elements read by this thread are: `row_in[start + tid*4], row_in[start + (tid+BLOCK)*4], ...` = `row_in[start + tid*4], row_in[start + tid*4 + BLOCK*4], ...`. This is a strided read pattern, NOT coalesced! Threads within a warp read from addresses that are BLOCK*4 = 1024 floats apart. This is terrible for memory coalescing. For coalesced reads, consecutive threads should read consecutive addresses. So the correct pattern is: ```cuda for (int i = tid; i < len; i += BLOCK) { float v = row_in[start + i]; heap_try(lv, li, &hs, k, v, start + i); } ``` This gives coalesced reads because threads 0-31 read from addresses `row_in[start], row_in[start+1], ..., row_in[start+31]`. But for float4 loads to be coalesced, I need threads to read consecutive float4s: ```cuda for (int i4 = tid; i4 < n4; i4 += BLOCK) { float4 f = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(row_in)[start/4 + i4]; ... } ``` Wait, but `start/4 + i4` might not give coalesced reads either. For coalesced float4 reads, consecutive threads should read consecutive float4 values. So thread 0 reads float4[0], thread 1 reads float4[1], etc. This means: ```cuda for (int i4 = tid; i4 < n4; i4 += BLOCK) { float4 f = row4[start/4 + i4]; // Wait, start might not be a multiple of 4... but chunk_size is 1024, so start is always a multiple of 4. } ``` Actually, `row4` is already `reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(row_in)`, and `start/4 + i4` gives the float4 index. Thread tid reads float4 at index `start/4 + tid`, and threads read consecutive float4s: `start/4 + 0, start/4 + 1, ..., start/4 + 31`. This IS coalesced. But wait, in my original kernel (not the scan kernel), I have: ```cuda const float4* row4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(row_in); int n4 = n >> 2; for (int i4 = tid; i4 < n4; i4 += BLOCK) { float4 f = row4[i4]; ``` Here, thread tid reads `row4[tid]`, thread tid+1 reads `row4[tid+1]`, etc. Within a warp, threads 0-31 read `row4[0], row4[1], ..., row4[31]`. This is coalesced! In the scan kernel, the indexing is different. Let me re-check: ```cuda const float4* r4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(row_in + start); int n4 = len >> 2; for (int i4 = tid; i4 < n4; i4 += BLOCK) { float4 f = r4[i4]; int base = start + (i4 << 2); ``` Here, `r4` points to `row_in + start`. Thread tid reads `r4[tid]`, which is `(row_in + start)[tid * 4]` = `row_in[start + tid * 4]`. Thread tid+BLOCK reads `r4[tid + BLOCK]` = `row_in[start + (tid + BLOCK) * 4]`. Within a warp, threads 0-31 read: `row_in[start + 0], row_in[start + 4], ..., row_in[start + 124]`. This is NOT coalesced! Consecutive threads read from addresses 16 bytes apart (4 floats). For coalesced reads, consecutive threads should read from consecutive 4-byte addresses. For the single-kernel version, thread tid reads `row4[tid]` = `row_in[tid * 4]`. Within a warp, threads 0-31 read `row_in[0], row_in[4], ..., row_in[124]`. Same issue! Wait, actually, for the single-kernel version: - `row4[tid]` = the float4 at index `tid`, which reads 4 floats starting at `row_in[tid * 4]`. - But `row4[tid + BLOCK]` = the float4 at index `tid + BLOCK`, which reads 4 floats starting at `row_in[(tid + BLOCK) * 4]`. So the iteration pattern is: thread 0 reads float4[0], float4[256], float4[512], ... This means thread 0 reads floats 0-3, 1024-1027, 2048-2051, etc. And thread 1 reads floats 4-7, 1028-1031, etc. Within a warp (threads 0-31): - First iteration: read float4[0], float4[1], ..., float4[31] = 128 consecutive floats. - This is 128 floats = 512 bytes, in 16 memory transactions of 32 bytes each. Coalesced! Second iteration: read float4[256], float4[257], ..., float4[287] = another 128 consecutive floats. Also coalesced. So the float4 load pattern IS coalesced. The float4 loads give 4x throughput compared to scalar loads. Now for the scan kernel: - Thread tid reads float4 at index `tid` relative to `r4` (which starts at `row_in + start`). - Within a warp: float4[start_offset + 0], ..., float4[start_offset + 31]. This is also coalesced. OK, so the load pattern is fine. The issue must be elsewhere. Let me think about what else could cause 788μs for shape 0 (batch=1, n=131072, k=64). +// ---------- sequential merge (thread 0, for small k) ---------- //
+
+__device__ void seq_merge_topk(
+    const float* sv, const int* si, int total,
+    float* __restrict__ ov, int64_t* __restrict__ oi, int k)
+{
+    float best[64];
+    int best_i[64];
+    int bs = 0;
+    for (int i = 0; i < total; i++)
+        heap_try(best, best_i, &bs, k, sv[i], si[i]);
+    for (int i = bs; i < k; i++) { best[i] = -FLT_MAX; best_i[i] = 0; }
+    // Extract descending
+    for (int i = k - 1; i >= 0; i--) {
+        ov[i] = best[0];
+        oi[i] = static_cast<int64_t>(best_i[0]);
+        bs--;
+        if (bs > 0) {
+            best[0] = best[bs]; best_i[0] = best_i[bs];
+            sift_down(best, best_i, bs, 0);
+        }
+    }
+}

For batch=1, we need 1 block. With 1 block on 1 SM, we get at most 1536 threads. Using BLOCK=256: 256 threads, 8 warps. Memory bandwidth per SM: ~100KB / 102400 * 1800 GB/s ≈ 1.76 TB/s? No, bandwidth is not proportional to shared memory. The DRAM bandwidth is shared across all SMs. A single SM's memory throughput depends on the memory controller. Actually, for a single SM, the memory throughput is limited by the SM's ability to issue memory requests. With 8 warps and 2 warp schedulers, each issuing 1 memory instruction per cycle, the SM can issue ~2 * 32 * 4 = 256 bytes per cycle. At 2.6 GHz, that's ~665 GB/s. So a single SM can achieve up to ~665 GB/s of memory bandwidth, which is ~37% of peak. For batch=1, n=131072, k=64: the kernel time would be dominated by the sequential merge (thread 0 merging 128*64=8192 candidates in shared memory). I need a parallel merge for large k. Let me implement the bitonic sort merge in shared memory. The __syncthreads() overhead is significant (78-91 syncs for k=32-64), but it should be faster than the sequential merge for large k. For k=32, BLOCK=256: N = 256*32 = 8192. Bitonic sort: 13*14/2 = 91 steps. 91 * 50ns sync = 4.6μs. Plus 91 * 16 * 1ns = 1.5μs compute. Total: 6.1μs. Sequential merge: 10μs. Bitonic sort: 6.1μs. Bitonic sort is faster! For k=64, BLOCK=128: N = 128*64 = 8192. Same: 6.1μs. For k=16, BLOCK=256: N = 256*16 = 4096. Bitonic: 12*13/2=78 steps. 78*50ns=3.9μs. Plus 78*8*1ns=0.6μs. Total: 4.5μs. Sequential merge: 4.3μs. About the same. So use bitonic sort for k >= 16, sequential merge for k < 16. Let me implement this with a runtime dispatch. Actually, let me try an even better approach: instead of bitonic sort (which has O(N log^2 N) comparisons and many syncs), use a parallel heap-based selection. **Parallel heap selection:** 1. All threads write their local top-k to shared memory 2. Multiple threads participate in the merge: a. Thread 0-31 each maintain their own "merge heap" of size k b. Thread t processes candidates t, t+32, t+64, ... (strided) c. Each thread inserts its assigned candidates into its merge heap d. After all candidates are processed, thread 0 merges the 32 merge heaps This is like a reduce operation. The total work is the same as sequential merge (BLOCK*k comparisons), but distributed across 32 threads. So 32x faster for the merge step. For k=64, BLOCK=128: - 128*64 = 8192 candidates, 32 threads - Each thread: 8192/32 = 256 candidates, insert into heap of 64 - Per thread: 256 * 1 + ~16*6 = 352 comparisons ≈ 0.4μs - Final merge of 32 heaps: 32*64 = 2048 candidates, thread 0: ~2048*1 + ~30*6 = 2228 ≈ 2.2μs - Total: 0.4 + 2.2 = 2.6μs Wait, the per-thread merge takes 0.4μs, and all 32 threads run in parallel. So the parallel phase takes 0.4μs. Then the sequential final merge takes 2.2μs. Total: 2.6μs. Much better than the pure sequential merge (10μs) or bitonic sort (6.1μs)! But can I do even better? What if I use a hierarchical approach: - Phase 1: 128 threads each merge 64 candidates into heaps of k (8192/128 = 64 per thread) - Per thread: 64 * 1 + ~4*6 = 88 comparisons ≈ 0.09μs - Parallel: 0.09μs - Need __syncthreads() between phase 1 and phase 2 - Phase 2: 1 thread merges 128*k candidates (128*64=8192) - This is the same as the original sequential merge! Hmm, that doesn't help because phase 2 is still sequential. Better hierarchical approach: - Phase 1: Each warp reduces its 32*k candidates to k (warp shuffle merge) - Per thread: 5 rounds * k shuffles + heap operations ≈ 2μs for k=64 - All warps in parallel: 2μs - But this has the register pressure issue... OK, let me try the "parallel heap selection" approach with 32 threads. This avoids warp shuffles and register pressure: ```cuda // Phase 1: 32 threads each merge BLOCK*k/32 candidates __shared__ float merge_vals[32][64]; __shared__ int merge_idxs[32][64]; int merge_threads = 32; int stride = merge_threads; int per_thread = (BLOCK * k + stride - 1) / stride; float mv[64]; int mi[64]; int ms = 0; for (int i = tid; i < BLOCK * k; i += stride) { heap_try(mv, mi, &ms, k, sv[i], si[i]); } // Write merge heaps to shared memory for (int i = 0; i < k; i++) { merge_vals[tid][i] = mv[i]; merge_idxs[tid][i] = mi[i]; } __syncthreads(); // Phase 2: Thread 0 merges 32 heaps if (tid == 0) { // Merge 32 * k = 2048 candidates ... } ``` But this uses 32 * 64 * 8 = 16KB of additional shared memory for the merge buffers. Plus 128*64*8 = 64KB for the initial write. Total: 80KB. Under the 99KB limit. Wait, but each of the 32 merge threads also needs 128 registers for their local heap (k=64). 32 * 128 = 4096 registers. Plus the other 96 threads that are idle. Total: 128 * (some registers) ≈ 128 * 50 = 6400. Fine. But only 32 threads do the merge, and the other 96 are idle. With only 32 active threads (1 warp), the SM has low occupancy during the merge phase. This might not be a problem since the merge reads from shared memory (fast). Actually, the issue is that each merge thread also needs to read from the shared memory buffer (sv, si). They need to read BLOCK*k = 8192 elements. With 32 threads, each reads 256 elements. At shared memory speed (~4 bytes/cycle), 256 * 4 = 1024 cycles ≈ 0.5μs. Plus heap operations: ~88 comparisons ≈ 0.09μs. Total phase 1: ~0.6μs. Phase 2: thread 0 merges 32 * 64 = 2048 candidates. Sequential: ~2048 + ~30*6 = 2228 comparisons ≈ 2.2μs. Total merge: 0.6 + 2.2 = 2.8μs. But wait, this approach has all 128 threads writing their heaps to shared memory, then 32 threads doing the merge. The write phase needs BLOCK*k shared memory. The merge phase needs 32*k additional shared memory. Total shared memory: BLOCK*k*8 + 32*k*8 = (BLOCK+32)*k*8 = (128+32)*64*8 = 160*512 = 81920 = 80KB. Under the 99KB limit. Let me implement this. But actually, the 32 merge threads each have their own local heap (64+64=128 registers). The compiler might not be able to keep these in registers for the 96 idle threads. Wait, the idle threads don't execute the merge code (they skip it with an `if (tid < 32)` check). So the compiler should optimize away their register usage. Hmm, actually, CUDA compiles a single code path for all threads. The registers are allocated for the worst case. So even idle threads consume registers for the merge heaps. This means 128 threads * 128 registers = 16384 registers. With 65536 per SM, that allows 4 blocks per SM. But with 80KB shared memory per block, only 1 block per SM. So occupancy is limited to 1 block = 128 threads = 4 warps. Low, but OK for shared memory operations. Wait, actually, the compiler is smart enough to see that the merge code is only executed by threads 0-31, and can optimize the register allocation. But CUDA doesn't do this optimization - all threads in a block share the same register allocation. So the 128 registers for the merge heap are allocated for ALL 128 threads, even though only 32 use them. This means 128 * 128 = 16384 registers. With 65536 per SM, 4 blocks per SM. But shared memory limits to 1 block. So 128 threads per SM = 4 warps. Low. Alternative: use only the first warp (threads 0-31) for the merge, and have the other threads wait at a barrier. This way, only 32 threads need the merge heap registers. But in CUDA, all threads in a block share the same register allocation. Even if threads 32-127 never execute the merge code, they still have the register space allocated. I could split this into two kernels: one for the scan, one for the merge. But that adds kernel launch overhead. Or I could use shared memory for the merge heaps instead of registers. This eliminates the register pressure but adds shared memory usage and slower access. For 32 merge threads with k=64: 32 * 64 * 8 = 16KB shared memory. Plus the initial buffer: 128*64*8 = 64KB. Total: 80KB. Under limit. But accessing shared memory is slower than registers (~5 cycles vs ~1 cycle). For the merge heap operations (frequent sift_down), this could be a 5x slowdown. Let me try a different approach: instead of per-thread merge heaps, use a single shared heap that all threads update cooperatively. **Cooperative shared heap approach:** 1. All threads write their local top-k to shared memory 2. Build a min-heap of size k in shared memory from the first k candidates 3. Each thread processes its assigned candidates (BLOCK*k / BLOCK = k per thread): a. Compare with heap root b. If larger, replace root and sift down 4. Each update requires atomic access or serialization The problem is concurrent access to the shared heap. Multiple threads updating the heap simultaneously would corrupt it. I can use a mutex (atomic compare-and-swap) to serialize updates: ```cuda __shared__ int mutex; if (tid == 0) mutex = 0; __syncthreads(); for (int i = tid; i < BLOCK * k; i += BLOCK) { float v = sv[i]; if (v > heap_vals[0]) { // Acquire lock while (atomicCAS(&mutex, 0, 1) != 0); // Double-check (value might have changed) if (v > heap_vals[0]) { heap_vals[0] = v; heap_idxs[0] = si[i]; sift_down_shared(heap_vals, heap_idxs, k, 0); } // Release lock atomicExch(&mutex, 0); } } ``` This is correct but has high contention. For 128 threads all trying to update the same heap, most will spin waiting for the lock. For random data, the probability of a candidate being larger than the root decreases as the heap fills. After the heap has the top k, most candidates will be smaller and won't need the lock. So contention decreases over time. Expected number of lock acquisitions: ~k * ln(BLOCK) ≈ 64 * 7 = 448 (for 128 threads, k=64). Each lock acquisition: ~1 atomicCAS ≈ ~100 cycles ≈ 50ns. 448 * 50ns = 22μs. Too slow! Let me try yet another approach. What about using warp-level primitives to aggregate updates? Actually, I think the best approach for this problem is to use a **bitonic sort** in shared memory. Despite the sync overhead, it's well-understood and parallel. For k=64, BLOCK=128, N=8192: - 91 steps, each with __syncthreads() - Each step: 8192/2=4096 comparisons, 128 threads do 32 each - Per step: 32 comparisons ≈ 32ns compute + 50ns sync ≈ 82ns - Total: 91 * 82ns = 7.5μs Actually, I estimated __syncthreads() at 50ns, but on Blackwell it might be different. Let me check. __syncthreads() typically takes 20-100 cycles depending on the architecture and the number of warps. On Blackwell with 4 warps per block, it might take ~50 cycles ≈ 25ns. 91 * (32ns + 25ns) = 91 * 57ns = 5.2μs. Better than my earlier estimate. Let me try the bitonic sort approach. Wait, but there's a problem. N = BLOCK * k might not be a power of 2 for all k values. The bitonic sort works cleanly. Now, shared memory for bitonic sort: BLOCK * k * 8 bytes. - k=64, BLOCK=128: 65536 = 64KB ✓ (under 99KB) - k=32, BLOCK=256: 65536 = 64KB ✓ - k=16, BLOCK=256: 32768 = 32KB ✓ - k=8, BLOCK=256: 16384 = 16KB ✓ - k=1, BLOCK=256: 2048 = 2KB ✓ All under the 99KB opt-in limit. Let me implement the bitonic sort merge. For the bitonic sort of N elements: ```cuda for (int size = 2; size <= N; size <<= 1) { for (int stride = size >> 1; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { for (int idx = tid; idx < N >> 1; idx += BLOCK) { int a = (idx / stride) * (stride << 1) + (idx % stride); int b = a + stride; bool descending = (a & size) == 0; bool swap = descending ? (sv[a] < sv[b]) : (sv[a] > sv[b]); if (swap) { float tv = sv[a]; sv[a] = sv[b]; sv[b] = tv; int ti = si[a]; si[a] = si[b]; si[b] = ti; } } __syncthreads(); } } ``` Wait, I need to verify the indexing. It builds bitonic sequences and then sorts them. A bitonic sequence is one that first increases then decreases (or can be circularly shifted to have this property). For example, [3,7,9,6] is bitonic (increases 3→7→9, then decreases 9→6). The bitonic sort algorithm: 1. For k=2: creates sorted pairs (alternating directions) 2. For k=4: creates sorted subsequences of size 4 (alternating directions) 3. etc. The merge step for size k takes two sorted subsequences of size k/2 (one ascending, one descending) and creates a sorted subsequence of size k. import torch
import time

# Compile with verbose output to see register usage
from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline

CUDA_SRC = r'''
#include <torch/extension.h>
#include <c10/cuda/CUDAException.h>
#include <cfloat>

__device__ __forceinline__
void sift_up(float* v, int* idx, int pos) {
    while (pos > 0) {
        int p = (pos - 1) >> 1;
        if (v[pos] >= v[p]) break;
        float tv = v[pos]; v[pos] = v[p]; v[p] = tv;
        int ti = idx[pos]; idx[pos] = idx[p]; idx[p] = ti;
        pos = p;
    }
}

__device__ __forceinline__
void sift_down(float* v, int* idx, int sz, int pos) {
    while (true) {
        int l = (pos << 1) + 1;
        int r = l + 1;
        int s = pos;
        if (l < sz && v[l] < v[s]) s = l;
        if (r < sz && v[r] < v[s]) s = r;
        if (s == pos) break;
        float tv = v[pos]; v[pos] = v[s]; v[s] = tv;
        int ti = idx[pos]; idx[pos] = idx[s]; idx[s] = ti;
        pos = s;
    }
}

__device__ __forceinline__
void heap_try(float* v, int* idx, int* hs, int k, float val, int id) {
    if (*hs < k) {
        int p = (*hs)++;
        v[p] = val; idx[p] = id;
        sift_up(v, idx, p);
    } else if (val > v[0]) {
        v[0] = val; idx[0] = id;
        sift_down(v, idx, k, 0);
    }
}

template<int BLOCK>
__global__ void topk_kernel(
    const float* __restrict__ input,
    float* __restrict__ out_vals,
    int64_t* __restrict__ out_idxs,
    int n, int k)
{
    int row = blockIdx.x;
    int tid = threadIdx.x;
    const float* row_in = input + (int64_t)row * n;

    float lv[64]; int li[64]; int hs = 0;

    const float4* row4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(row_in);
    int n4 = n >> 2;
    for (int i4 = tid; i4 < n4; i4 += BLOCK) {
        float4 f = row4[i4];
        int base = i4 << 2;
        heap_try(lv, li, &hs, k, f.x, base);
        heap_try(lv, li, &hs, k, f.y, base + 1);
        heap_try(lv, li, &hs, k, f.z, base + 2);
        heap_try(lv, li, &hs, k, f.w, base + 3);
    }
    int rem = n4 << 2;
    for (int i = rem + tid; i < n; i += BLOCK)
        heap_try(lv, li, &hs, k, row_in[i], i);
    for (int i = hs; i < k; i++) { lv[i] = -FLT_MAX; li[i] = -1; }

    extern __shared__ char smem[];
    float* sv = (float*)smem;
    int* si = (int*)(sv + BLOCK * k);
    for (int i = 0; i < k; i++) { sv[tid*k+i] = lv[i]; si[tid*k+i] = li[i]; }
    __syncthreads();

    if (tid == 0) {
        float best[64]; int best_i[64]; int bs = 0;
        for (int i = 0; i < BLOCK * k; i++)
            heap_try(best, best_i, &bs, k, sv[i], si[i]);
        for (int i = bs; i < k; i++) { best[i] = -FLT_MAX; best_i[i] = 0; }
        float* ov = out_vals + (int64_t)row * k;
        int64_t* oi = out_idxs + (int64_t)row * k;
        for (int i = k-1; i >= 0; i--) {
            ov[i] = best[0]; oi[i] = (int64_t)best_i[0]; bs--;
            if (bs > 0) { best[0] = best[bs]; best_i[0] = best_i[bs]; sift_down(best, best_i, bs, 0); }
        }
    }
}

std::tuple<torch::Tensor, torch::Tensor> launch_topk(torch::Tensor x, int k) {
    int B = x.size(0), N = x.size(1);
    auto out_v = torch::empty({B, k}, x.options());
    auto out_i = torch::empty({B, k}, x.options().dtype(torch::kInt64));
    int BLOCK = (k <= 32) ? 256 : 128;
    int smem = BLOCK * k * (sizeof(float) + sizeof(int));
    cudaFuncSetAttribute(topk_kernel<128>, cudaFuncAttributeMaxDynamicSharedMemorySize, 128*64*8);
    cudaFuncSetAttribute(topk_kernel<256>, cudaFuncAttributeMaxDynamicSharedMemorySize, 256*32*8);
    if (BLOCK == 256)
        topk_kernel<256><<<B, 256, smem>>>(x.data_ptr<float>(), out_v.data_ptr<float>(), out_i.data_ptr<int64_t>(), N, k);
    else
        topk_kernel<128><<<B, 128, smem>>>(x.data_ptr<float>(), out_v.data_ptr<float>(), out_i.data_ptr<int64_t>(), N, k);
    C10_CUDA_CHECK(cudaGetLastError());
    return std::make_tuple(out_v, out_i);
}
'''

mod = load_inline(
    name='topk_profile',
    cpp_sources='std::tuple<torch::Tensor, torch::Tensor> launch_topk(torch::Tensor x, int k)

import torch

# ncu launch
import subprocess, sys, os
exe = sys.executable
script = '''
import torch, solution
x = torch.randn(64, 8192, device=\"cuda\", dtype=torch.float32)
for _ in range(3): solution._mod.launch_topk(x, 8)
'''
with open('/tmp/ncu_test.py', 'w') as f:
    f.write(script)

r = subprocess.run(
    ['ncu', '--set', 'full', '--target-processes', 'all', '-c', '2',
     '--python-version', 'true', exe, '/tmp/ncu_test.py'],
    capture_output=True, text=True, timeout=60, env={**os.environ, 'CUDA_LAUNCH_BLOCKING': '1'}
)
# Extract key metrics

    if any(k in line for k in ['register', 'shared', 'block', 'grid', 'throughput', 'DRAM', 'Duration', 'Launch', 'topk']):
        print(line)
if r.returncode != 0:
    print('STDERR:', r.stderr[-2000:] if len(r.stderr) > 2000 else r.stderr)

Interesting! The ncu output reveals important details: For the topk_kernel<256> (batch=64, k=8): - **Duration**: 63.52μs (for one kernel launch? Or averaged?) - **DRAM Throughput**: 2.18% (very low) - **Memory Throughput**: 37.32 GB/s - **Registers Per Thread**: 40 (very reasonable for k=8!) - **Shared memory bank conflicts**: 39-48% of shared loads/stores have bank conflicts! The bank conflicts are a major issue! The shared memory write pattern is: ```cuda for (int i = 0; i < k; i++) { sv[tid * k + i] = lv[i]; si[tid * k + i] = li[i]; } ``` For k=8, thread tid writes to sv[tid*8], sv[tid*8+1], ..., sv[tid*8+7]. Threads 0-31 (a warp) write to sv[0..255]. Each float is 4 bytes. Shared memory has 32 banks, each 4 bytes wide. So sv[0] is in bank 0, sv[1] is in bank 1, ..., sv[31] is in bank 31, sv[32] is in bank 0, etc. For thread 0: writes to sv[0], sv[1], ..., sv[7]. These are in banks 0-7. ✓ For thread 1: writes to sv[8], sv[9], ..., sv[15]. These are in banks 8-15. ✓ ... For thread 3: writes to sv[24], ..., sv[31]. Banks 24-31. ✓ For thread 4: writes to sv[32], ..., sv[39]. Banks 0-7. Same as thread 0! So threads 0 and 4 write to the same banks! This causes bank conflicts. The fix: use a padded shared memory layout to avoid bank conflicts. Or change the access pattern. For the write pattern, threads within a warp access sv[tid*k], sv[tid*k+1], ..., sv[tid*k+k-1]. If k=8, threads 0-3 access banks 0-31 (unique), threads 4-7 access banks 0-31 (same as 0-3). This causes 8-way bank conflicts for k=8. For the bitonic sort merge, the access pattern is different and depends on the bitonic sort's indexing. But wait, in the current solution (v5 with bitonic sort), the shared memory is used for both the initial write and the bitonic sort. The bank conflicts during the sort might be worse. Let me think about how to fix this. For the initial write: - Instead of writing tid*k + i, I can write i*BLOCK + tid. This way, threads write to consecutive addresses (sv[0], sv[1], ..., sv[31] for a warp), which is bank-conflict-free! But then the heap for thread tid is stored at sv[0*BLOCK+tid], sv[1*BLOCK+tid], ..., sv[(k-1)*BLOCK+tid]. The distance between consecutive elements of the same thread's heap is BLOCK (256) positions. This makes the heap operations slower (non-sequential access). Alternative: Transpose the layout. Write in a coalesced pattern, then read in a thread-friendly pattern. But this requires an extra transpose step. Actually, for the sequential merge by thread 0, the access pattern during the merge is sequential (sv[0], sv[1], ..., sv[BLOCK*k-1]). This is fine regardless of the layout. The bank conflicts only matter during the write phase. Let me try transposing the write: each thread writes its heap at offset i*BLOCK + tid. ```cuda for (int i = 0; i < k; i++) { sv[i * BLOCK + tid] = lv[i]; si[i * BLOCK + tid] = li[i]; } ``` This is bank-conflict-free because threads 0-31 write to sv[i*256+0], sv[i*256+1], ..., sv[i*256+31], which are in consecutive banks. Then thread 0 reads all elements sequentially: ```cuda for (int i = 0; i < BLOCK * k; i++) heap_try(best, best_i, &bs, k, sv[i], si[i]); ``` This is also sequential and bank-conflict-free. The compiler might optimize them away for threads that don't enter the block. But in CUDA, all threads share the same register allocation. So the compiler allocates registers for the worst case. With best[64] + best_i[64] + lv[64] + li[64] = 256 array elements = 256 registers. But the GPU max is 255 registers per thread! The compiler would need to spill to local memory. Wait, but the compiler is smart enough to see that lv/li are only used before the __syncthreads(), and best/best_i are only used after. So it can reuse the registers. lv/li occupy the same registers as best/best_i. Let me check: the code flow is: 1. Declare lv[64], li[64], hs=0 2. Scan elements using lv, li 3. Write to shared memory using lv, li 4. __syncthreads() 5. (if tid==0) Declare best[64], best_i[64], bs=0 6. Read from shared memory, write to best, best_i 7. Output from best, best_i The compiler can see that lv/li are not used after step 3, and best/best_i are not used before step 5. So they can share the same registers. Total: 64+64 = 128 registers for the arrays, plus ~20 for other variables = ~148 registers. But the ncu output shows only 40-60 registers! Wait, but the heap operations (sift_up, sift_down) access elements up to index k-1. For k=8, the maximum index is 7. The sift_down compares with left child at 2*pos+1, which for pos=0 is 1, for pos=1 is 3, for pos=3 is 7. So the maximum accessed index for k=8 is 7. The compiler can optimize the array to size 8. But the arrays are declared as size 64 (MAX_K), not size k (runtime). The compiler can't optimize based on runtime k. So it must allocate all 64 elements. Unless... the compiler inlines the heap functions and sees that the array is only accessed up to index k-1 = 7 for k=8, and optimizes the rest away. But k is a runtime parameter, so the compiler can't know this at compile time. Hmm, but the template parameter MAX_K is 64, not k. And k is a function parameter. The compiler generates code that works for any k from 1 to 64. So it must be prepared for k=64 and allocate all 64 elements. With 64 + 64 = 128 array elements + ~20 other = 148 registers. But ncu shows only 40-60. This means the compiler is spilling the arrays to local memory! That's the problem! The arrays are too large for registers, so the compiler spills them to local memory (which is actually global memory). Every heap operation involves reading from and writing to global memory, which is extremely slow. For k=8, the arrays are 64 floats + 64 ints = 128 elements. At 4 bytes each, that's 512 bytes per thread. With 256 threads per block, that's 128KB of local memory per block. At 1.8TB/s, reading 128KB takes ~71μs. Wait, but local memory is cached in L1/L2. So the actual access time depends on the cache behavior. With good caching, it might be faster. But for random access patterns (heap operations), the caching is poor. This explains the poor performance! The compiler is spilling the heap arrays to local memory because they don't fit in registers. The fix: declare the arrays with the actual size k, not MAX_K. But since k is a runtime parameter, I can't use it as an array size in CUDA. Alternative: Template on k. ```cuda template<int BLOCK, int K> __global__ void topk_kernel(...) { float lv[K]; int li[K]; ... float best[K]; int best_i[K]; ... } ``` This way, for k=8, the arrays are only 8 elements, and the compiler can keep them in registers (8+8 = 16 registers for the heap). For k=64, the arrays are 64 elements (64+64 = 128 registers). This might still spill, but it's the minimum needed. Let me implement this by templating on k and instantiating for k=1, 8, 16, 32, 64. Actually, I can template on K and instantiate at runtime based on k: ```cpp if (k == 1) topk_kernel<256, 1><<<B, 256, smem>>>(...); else if (k == 8) topk_kernel<256, 8><<<B, 256, smem>>>(...); ... ``` This is clean and allows the compiler to optimize for each k value. Let me implement this. It's a significant change but should dramatically improve performance.
in 0out 0cache_r 0cache_w 0
assistant
Key insight from ncu: the arrays `lv[64]`, `li[64]`, `best[64]`, `best_i[64]` are being spilled to local memory because they're declared as size 64 regardless of runtime k. For k=8, the compiler allocates 128+ registers and spills. I need to template on k to get proper register allocation.
